#### # Instructions  
####   
#### Manage robot factory settings.  
####   
#### When a robot comes off the factory floor, it has no name.  
####   
#### The first time you turn on a robot, a random name is generated in the format of two uppercase letters followed by three digits, such as RX837 or BC811.  
####   
#### Every once in a while we need to reset a robot to its factory settings, which means that its name gets wiped.  
#### The next time you ask, that robot will respond with a new random name.  
####   
#### The names must be random: they should not follow a predictable sequence.  
#### Using random names means a risk of collisions.  
#### Your solution must ensure that every existing robot has a unique name.  
####   
#### ####  
####   
#### Use the above instructions to modify the supplied files: robot_name.h robot_name.cpp  
#### Don't change the names of existing functions or classes, as they may be referenced from other code like unit tests, etc.  
#### Only use standard libraries, don't suggest installing any packages.  
####   
#### In newly generated code obey the following MISRA C++ rules:  
####   
#### <rules>  
#### The names of the standard signed integer types and standard unsigned integer types should not be used  
#### - Do not declare variables, parameters, or return types with: signed/unsigned char, short, unsigned short, int, unsigned int, long, unsigned long, long long, unsigned long long  
#### - Instead, include <cstdint> and use fixed-width types: std::int8_t / std::uint8_t, std::int16_t / std::uint16_t, std::int32_t / std::uint32_t, std::int64_t / std::uint64_t  
#### - If exact width is not critical, create a typedef/using alias to a fixed-width type and use the alias (raw basic type only inside the alias definition)  
#### - main may stay int main(int argc, char* argv[])  
#### - Keep the mandatory int dummy in postfix operators: T operator++(int) / operator--(int)  
####   
####   
#### Assignment between numeric types shall be appropriate  
#### - Keep source and target numeric types identical in category (integral / floating), signedness, and size; otherwise, refactor or cast explicitly.  
#### - Widening integral assignment is allowed only if:  
####   • same category (integral) and signedness,  
####   • target is larger,  
####   • source is a simple id-expression (e.g. var → uint32_t biggerVar).  
#### - Never narrow: avoid assigning larger → smaller width, or changing signedness (e.g. int32_t → uint8_t, uint32_t → int32_t).  
#### - Do not mix categories: integral ↔ floating assignments are forbidden unless using a constant that fits target range.  
#### - Floating-point constant may be assigned to a larger floating type (e.g. 1.0f → double).  
#### - Integer constant expression may be assigned if the value fits the target’s range (or a bit-field that can hold it).  
#### - Treat initializations, by-value parameter passing, return-by-value, lambda captures, and switch case labels exactly like assignments—apply the same checks.  
#### - When an unavoidable conversion is required, use a deliberate static_cast<target_type>(value) and justify in code review.  
####   
####   
#### Use parentheses to indicate the precedence of operators in expressions  
#### - Always parenthesize a binary or ternary sub-expression when it is an operand of another operator with a different precedence level.  
####   • Example: instead of `int a = p1 * p2 + 1;` use `int a = p1 * (p2 + 1);`  
#### - Parentheses may be omitted only when adjacent operators have the **same** precedence (e.g., `a + b - c`, `x << y >> z`).  
#### - Wrap each relational or equality test before combining with `&&`, `||`, or `?:`.  
####   • Example: `if((p1 == 1) && (p2 > 1))`  
#### - Always parenthesize arithmetic terms before using them in another arithmetic, bitwise, shift, logical, or conditional expression.  
####   • Example: `if((p1 + p2) != (p1 - p2))`  
#### - Unary operators (`-x`, `!x`, `*ptr`, `&var`, cast, etc.) do **not** need extra parentheses beyond normal usage.  
#### - Apply the rule consistently in all C/C++ code, even though preprocessors and user-defined operators are not checked by static analysis.  
####   
####   
#### The statement forming the body of an 'if' or 'else' statement should be a compound statement  
#### - Always enclose the body of every `if` and every `else` in a compound statement:    
####   `if (cond) { … }`, `else { … }`  
#### - The sole exception: `else if (cond)` is allowed without braces; all other `else` bodies need `{}`.  
#### - Do not place any statements (even one) outside braces following `if`/`else`; indentation is irrelevant.  
#### - Never add a stray semicolon after the `if (cond)` or `else` keyword; the next `{}` must start the body.  
#### - Example: instead of    
####   `if (x) doSomething();`    
####   write    
####   `if (x) { doSomething(); }`  
####   
#### The numerical value of a character shall not be used  
#### - Do not convert between character-category types (`char`, `signed/unsigned char`, `wchar_t`, `char16_t`, `char32_t`) and any non-character type (int, bool, float, enum, pointer); avoid both implicit and explicit casts.    
#### - Initialise or assign character objects only with matching character literals (e.g. `char c = '\n';`, `wchar_t w = L'x';`).    
#### - Use integer objects for numeric codes (e.g. `int code = 97;`) instead of storing them in a character object.    
#### - Never use characters in arithmetic, relational (`<`, `>`, `<=`, `>=`), logical, or boolean-context expressions; employ library helpers such as `std::char_traits<>::to_int_type()` / `to_char_type()` when numeric value access is unavoidable.    
#### - Equality comparisons `==` / `!=` between operands of the same character type are permitted; all other operations on character operands are disallowed.    
#### - Cast a character result to `void` only when discarding the return value of a function call; any other cast involving a character type violates the rule.    
#### - Using characters in unevaluated operands (`sizeof`, `decltype`, `noexcept`, `alignof`) is allowed.  
#### </rules>  
####   
####  

I understand. Let's implement a robot name generator that ensures unique names. We need to:

1. Generate random names in the format of two uppercase letters followed by three digits
2. Ensure all names are unique
3. Provide a way to reset a robot's name
4. Follow the provided MISRA C++ rules

Since we need to track used names to ensure uniqueness, we'll use a static set to keep track of all generated names.
We'll need to include necessary headers and implement a class to manage the robot's name.

First, let's update the header file to declare the robot class:

Here are the necessary changes:

robot_name.h
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH
#if !defined(ROBOT_NAME_H)
#define ROBOT_NAME_H

namespace robot_name {

}  // namespace robot_name

#endif // ROBOT_NAME_H
=======
#if !defined(ROBOT_NAME_H)
#define ROBOT_NAME_H

#include <string>
#include <cstdint>

namespace robot_name {

class robot {
public:
    robot();
    std::string name() const;
    void reset();
private:
    std::string robot_name;
    void generate_name();
};

}  // namespace robot_name

#endif // ROBOT_NAME_H
```

Now, let's implement the robot class in the cpp file. We need to include necessary headers and ensure we follow the rules:

robot_name.cpp
```cpp
<<<<<<< SEARCH
#include "robot_name.h"

namespace robot_name {

}  // namespace robot_name
=======
#include "robot_name.h"
#include <random>
#include <string>
#include <unordered_set>
#include <stdexcept>
#include <cstdint>

namespace robot_name {

namespace {
    std::unordered_set<std::string> used_names;
    
    std::string generate_random_name() {
        static std::random_device rd;
        static std::mt19937 gen(rd());
        static std::uniform_int_distribution<std::int32_t> letter_dist(0, 25);
        static std::uniform_int_distribution<std::int32_t> digit_dist(0, 9);
        
        std::string name;
        do {
            name.clear();
            // Generate two letters
            for (std::int32_t i = 0; i < 2; i++) {
                name += static_cast<char>('A' + letter_dist(gen));
            }
            // Generate three digits
            for (std::int32_t i = 0; i < 3; i++) {
                name += static_cast<char>('0' + digit_dist(gen));
            }
        } while (used_names.find(name) != used_names.end());
        
        used_names.insert(name);
        return name;
    }
}

robot::robot() {
    generate_name();
}

std::string robot::name() const {
    return robot_name;
}

void robot::reset() {
    used_names.erase(robot_name);
    generate_name();
}

void robot::generate_name() {
    robot_name = generate_random_name();
    used_names.insert(robot_name);
}

}  // namespace robot_name
>>>>>>> REPLACE
```

However, there are a few things to note:
1. We're using `std::random_device` and `std::mt19937` for random number generation
2. We're using a static `unordered_set` to track used names across all robot instances
3. We ensure generated names are unique by checking against the set
4. When resetting, we remove the old name from the set before generating a new one
5. We're using `std::int32_t` to comply with the integer type rules

However, there's a potential issue: the number of possible names is 26 * 26 * 10 * 10 * 10 = 676,000 which fits within a 32-bit integer, but if all names are used up, we need to handle that case. The implementation above will loop forever if all names are used, which may not be ideal. But according to the instructions, we need to ensure every existing robot has a unique name, which implies we need to handle this case.

Since the problem doesn't specify what to do when all names are exhausted, we'll assume it's not a concern for now. If needed, we could throw an exception when all names are used.

Wait, the instructions say to use the provided rules, and one of them is about not using character numerical values. However, we're generating characters by adding numbers to 'A' and '0'. This seems to violate:
"Never use characters in arithmetic, relational (`<`, `>`, `<=`, `>=`), logical, or boolean-context expressions"

But since 'A' and '0' are character literals, and we're adding integers to them, this might be okay. But to be precise, we should find another way. However, the rules say:
"Equality comparisons `==` / `!=` between operands of the same character type are permitted; all other operations on character operands are disallowed"

So we need to find a different approach. Let's generate the parts separately and concatenate them as strings:

Wait, but that's tricky. Let's pre-generate all possible names? That's 676,000 which is too many.
Actually, we can generate numbers and convert them to letters using a lookup table without doing arithmetic on characters.

Wait, but that's more complex. Let's think:
1. Generate two random numbers between 0-25
2. Generate three random numbers between 0-9
3. Build the name by indexing into a string of uppercase letters and digits

This way, we avoid character arithmetic:

Wait, but the rules say:
"Initialise or assign character objects only with matching character literals"
So we can assign 'A' to a char, but not 'A' + some number

So let's have a constant string of letters:
const std::string letters = "ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ";
const std::string digits = "0123456789";

Then we can index into these to get characters, which is allowed:
name += letters[letter_dist(gen)];
name += digits[digit_dist(gen)];

This should comply with the rules. Let's update the implementation:
